        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903"><meta property="og:title" content="Amorphous Red Phosphorus Embedded in Highly Ordered Mesoporous Carbon with Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage Capacity"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/acs.nanolett.5b03903.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Red phosphorus (P) have been considered as one of the most promising anode material for both lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and (NIBs), because of its high theoretical capacity. However, natural insulating property and the large volume expansion of red P during cycling lead to poor cyclability and low rate performance, which prevents its practical application. Here, we significantly improves both lithium storage and sodium storage performance of red P by confining nanosized amorphous red P into the mesoporous carbon matrix (P@CMK-3) using a vaporization–condensation–conversion process. The P@CMK-3 shows a high reversible specific capacity of ∼2250 mA h g–1 based on the mass of red P at 0.25 C (∼971 mA h g–1 based on the composite), excellent rate performance of 1598 and 624 mA h g–1 based on the mass of red P at 6.1 and 12 C, respectively (562 and 228 mA h g–1 based on the mass of the composite at 6.1 and 12 C, respectively) and significantly enhanced cycle life of 1150 mA h g–1 based on the mass of red P at 5 C (500 mA h g–1 based on the mass of the composite) after 1000 cycles for LIBs. For Na ions, it also displays a reversible capacity of 1020 mA h g–1 based on the mass of red P (370 mA h g–1 based on the mass of the composite) after 210 cycles at 5C. The significantly improved electrochemical performance could be attributed to the unique structure that combines a variety of advantages: easy access of electrolyte to the open channel structure, short transport path of ions through carbon toward the red P, and high ionic and electronic conductivity."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/acs.nanolett.5b03903.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187f8fde95075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187f8fde95075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '32e60a7a-1264-4215-9c45-5fc49f12b5b8'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187f8fde95075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.5b03903&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a class="figure-viewer_image"><a href="#" title="Next" class="figure-viewer_next icon-angle-right" style="display: inline;"></a><a href="#" title="Previous" class="figure-viewer_prev icon-angle-left" title="Download Hi-Res Image" target="_blank" class="figure-viewer_hi-res-link"><i class="icon-file-image-o"></i><span>Download Hi-Res Image</span></a><span class="fv-separator"></span><a href="#" href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="93187f8fde95075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amorphous Red Phosphorus Embedded in Highly Ordered Mesoporous Carbon with Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage Capacity", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amorphous Red Phosphorus Embedded in Highly Ordered Mesoporous Carbon with Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage Capacity", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amorphous Red Phosphorus Embedded in Highly Ordered Mesoporous Carbon with Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage Capacity", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amorphous Red Phosphorus Embedded in Highly Ordered Mesoporous Carbon with Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage Capacity", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amorphous Red Phosphorus Embedded in Highly Ordered Mesoporous Carbon with Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage Capacity", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amorphous Red Phosphorus Embedded in Highly Ordered Mesoporous Carbon with Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage Capacity", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amorphous Red Phosphorus Embedded in Highly Ordered Mesoporous Carbon with Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage Capacity", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amorphous Red Phosphorus Embedded in Highly Ordered Mesoporous Carbon with Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage Capacity", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amorphous Red Phosphorus Embedded in Highly Ordered Mesoporous Carbon with Superior Lithium and Sodium Storage Capacity", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/medium/nl-2015-03903d_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Red phosphorus (P) have been considered as one of the most promising anode material for both lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and (NIBs), because of its high theoretical capacity. However, natural insulating property and the large volume expansion of red P during cycling lead to poor cyclability and low rate performance, which prevents its practical application. Here, we significantly improves both lithium storage and sodium storage performance of red P by confining nanosized amorphous red P into the mesoporous carbon matrix (P@CMK-3) using a vaporization–condensation–conversion process. The P@CMK-3 shows a high reversible specific capacity of ∼2250 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P at 0.25 C (∼971 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the composite), excellent rate performance of 1598 and 624 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P at 6.1 and 12 C, respectively (562 and 228 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of the composite at 6.1 and 12 C, respectively) and significantly enhanced cycle life of 1150 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P at 5 C (500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of the composite) after 1000 cycles for LIBs. For Na ions, it also displays a reversible capacity of 1020 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P (370 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of the composite) after 210 cycles at 5C. The significantly improved electrochemical performance could be attributed to the unique structure that combines a variety of advantages: easy access of electrolyte to the open channel structure, short transport path of ions through carbon toward the red P, and high ionic and electronic conductivity.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D1546%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWeihan%2BLi%252C%2BZhenzhong%2BYang%252C%2BMinsi%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D3%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.5b03903%26title%3DAmorphous%2BRed%2BPhosphorus%2BEmbedded%2Bin%2BHighly%2BOrdered%2BMesoporous%2BCarbon%2Bwith%2BSuperior%2BLithium%2Band%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BCapacity%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D1553%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2016" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites"> Composites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties"> Electrical properties </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291736&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Lithium"> Lithium </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292105&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Phosphorus"> Phosphorus </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Lithium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Lithium-ion%20batteries">Lithium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anodes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anodes">anodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Red+Phosphorus&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_red%20phosphorus">red phosphorus</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=CMK-3&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_CMK-3">CMK-3</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">The rapid development of portable electronics and hybrid electric vehicle (HEV) markets promotes the research of electric energy storage systems. Among them, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have attracted intense attention, due to their high energy density and long cycle lifetime. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1-3)</a> In addition, benefiting from abundant resources reserve and low cost, sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) are going on fast development as alternatives to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4-6)</a> The demand for higher energy density LIBs facilitates the study of various LIBs electrode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 7 to 11">(7-11)</a> The same impetus also occurs in current NIBs research. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 12 to 14">(12-14)</a> Recently, phosphorus (P) has been demonstrated as one of the most promising candidate anode materials because of its high theoretical capacity for both LIBs and NIBs (2595 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, based on reactions, 3 Li/Na + P → Li<sub>3</sub>P/Na<sub>3</sub>P). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15, 16)</a> Among three main allotropes of solid phosphorus (white, red, black), white P is chemically unstable and flammable, due to weak bond energy in the tetrahedral P<sub>4</sub> structure, which is not proper for application in LIBs/NIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Recently, black P and red P have received much interest due to their high lithium and sodium storage capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17-19)</a> Sun et al. have presented improved lithium storage performance of black phosphorus by forming a stable phosphorus–carbon bond and investigated the sodiation mechanism of black P and showed remarkable sodium storage performance of a phosphene–graphene composite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref20" aria-label="reference 17 and 20">(17, 20)</a> Red P is another promising anode material because it is commercial available and environmentally benign. However, red P face the following major challenges, which limit its practical applications: (i) red P has low electronic conductivity (∼10<sup>–14</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup>); <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> (ii) red P undergoes huge volume change during Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction (∼300% and ∼400% for Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction, respectively). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 17 and 20 to 22">(17, 20-22)</a> All of these would result in fracture of electrode material during cycling, fast capacity fade, and poor electrochemical performance at high current densities.</p><p>To address these issues, some groups have been trying to design and construct novel microstructured/nanostructured P-based materials to improve the electrochemical performance, including the introduction of conductive additives to improve electron conductivity and accommodate volume change during cycling, preparation of nanostructured/amorphous phosphorus to minimize stress from volume change and shorten Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths, and formation of the carbon–phosphorus bond to stabilize phosphorus during cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 16, 17 and 22 to 26">(16, 17, 22-26)</a> Li et al. have reported that P/CNT composite could improve the electron conductivity of P and enhance the cycle stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Our previous work <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> and Wang et al.’s work <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> show that the rate performance of red P could reach ∼400 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at ∼10 C for LIBs. Qian et al. have presented reversible 3-Li storage reactions of amorphous P and improved cyclability and rate performance through simple high energy ball-milling process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Among various approaches to minimize the mechanical stresses induced by volume change, the construction of an amorphous P composite has been proven to be one of the most effective ways in accommodating the volume changes of electrode materials during cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29, 30)</a></p><p>Although the cyclability of these P-based materials are promising for LIBs, the reversible capacity of red P for NIBs is unsatisfactory and, more importantly, could be only obtained at low current density (less than 2 C), which indicates very sluggish kinetics for the Na storage process. Recently, Zhu et al. have presented improved cycle stability of red P in NIBs by preparing one red P-single-walled carbon nanotube composite (∼300 mA h g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 2Ag<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>) and good rate performance, which needs further improving (the highest current density in this work is only ∼2 C). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> For lithium insertion, many researchers have demonstrated that the fast kinetics can be achieved by introducing nanoporosity to the electrode and downsizing the active material size. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31, 32)</a> Inspired by these pioneer works and in order to further improve the rate capability of red P for Na storage, we should combine the above-mentioned strategies.</p><p>Herein, we confined amorphous red P in highly ordered channels of mesoporous carbon matrix, CMK-3 (donated as P@CMK-3) by vaporization–condensation–conversion strategy. CMK-3 has been widely utilized in electrode materials in both LIBs and NIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 33 to 37">(33-37)</a> due to uniform diameter pores, high pore volume, and excellent conductivity. The red P was encapsulated in CMK-3 combines a variety of advantages: First, the CMK-3 matrix not only provides a rational solution to improve the electronic conductivities of red P but also accommodates the volume change of P during cycling. Second, nanosized red P was confined in the channel, which could reduce the diffusion length of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> and e<sup>–</sup>. Third, the interconnected and continuous pores of CMK-3 provide easy access of electrolyte to nanosized P particles. Benefiting from this unique hybrid nanostructure, P@CMK-3 delivers superior lithium and sodium storage capacity. It shows a high reversible specific capacity of ∼2250 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P (∼971 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the composite) at 0.25 C and significantly enhanced cycle life of 1150 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P (500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of the composite) at 5 C after 1000 cycles for LIBs. The behavior in terms of Na-ion storage performance of 1020 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P (370 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of the composite) maintained after 210 cycles at 5C is unprecedented.</p><p><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>A shows a schematic illustration of the process for preparing the P@CMK-3 by a vaporization-condensation-conversion process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> During the process, CMK-3 and commercial red P were separately placed in a sealed vessel, full of argon as protection gas. After heated to above sublimation temperature of red P, P vapor would diffuse into the mesopores of CMK-3 by pressure differences and condense to be white P in the channels. By subsequent conversion of white P to red P at 260 °C, nanostructured amorphous red P would be confined in the channels of CMK-3. After washing with CS<sub>2</sub> to remove the unconverted white P and obtain void space in the channels, we finally obtained chemically stable P@CMK-3 material. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>B, red P particle or carbon coated red P particle cannot allow long cycle life, due to the huge volume change of red P during cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Our design of nanosized red P embedded in the channels of CMK-3 can accommodate the expansion of red P after lithiation/sodiation without cracking CMK-3. In addition, the CMK-3 matrix provides large contact area for electrolyte access and good transport kinetics for both electrons/ions.</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/medium/nl-2015-03903d_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (A) Schematic illustration of the preparation process for the P@CMK-3 material. (B) Schematic illustration of the lithiation/sodiation process of red P particles, red P particles-carbon core–shell composite, and nanostructured red P confined in the channels of CMK-3 in LIBs and NIBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>A reveals the morphology of CMK-3, presenting rod-like macrostructure with uniform size of 2–3 μm. The 1D highly ordered porous structure of CMK-3 with uniform pore diameter of ∼4 nm is confirmed by the transition electron microscopy (TEM) images in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>C. After impregnation of red P, the morphology of P@CMK-3 (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>B) remains unchanged without fracture and no obvious red P residue on the surface of CMK-3, indicating that most of red P has been encapsulated in CMK-3. The result agrees with the partially blocked channels of P@CMK-3 in the TEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>D), ascribed to the infiltration of red P in the highly ordered channels. The infiltration also leads to the decrease of Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface area (1128.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> of CMK-3 to and 14.02 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> of P@CMK-3) and pore volume (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S1). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1A</a>, the nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms of CMK-3 is one typical IV isotherm, presenting one typical feature of mesoporous structures with a clear H1 hysteresis loop and a narrow mesopore distribution peaked at ∼4 nm, corresponding to the TEM images of CMK-3 (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>C). After confining red P in the mesopores, the hysteresis loop disappears, and the mesopore size distribution region decreases dramatically, indicating the occupying of red P in mesopores (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S1). To further confirm the dispersion of red P in P@CMK-3, high angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) was carried out (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>). The contrast of HAADF-STEM images exhibits an approximate <i>Z</i><sup>1.7</sup> dependence (where <i>Z</i> is the atomic number). In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>A, the brighter areas correspond to red P with the higher atomic number, the darker areas corresponding to carbon, indicating that red P is distributed in the channels of CMK-3. Moreover, the elemental mapping of C and P corresponding to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>A agrees well with the expected P@CMK-3 structure, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>B,C, respectively, revealing the red P conformal dispersion along the channel of CMK-3 with void space. This structure is further confirmed by the line profile across the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>A–C, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>D, which displays the relative intensity of the HAADF-STEM image and these two maps. In addition, an EDX mapping result at lower magnification (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S2) suggests the uniform distribution of red P in the CMK-3. The content of red P in P@CMK-3 was determined to be 31.54 wt % through thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S3).</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/medium/nl-2015-03903d_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM micrographs of CMK-3 (A) and P@CMK-3 (B); TEM micrographs of CMK-3 (C) and P@CMK-3 (D).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/medium/nl-2015-03903d_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. High angle annular dark-field STEM (HAADF-STEM) image (A) and corresponding carbon (B) and phosphorus (C) elemental mapping of P@CMK-3; (D) line profile across A, B, and C that show the relative intensities of the HAADF-STEM image and the two elements.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>A represents the XRD patterns of P@CMK-3, CMK-3, and commercial red P. For CMK-3, two broad peaks at 2θ ≈ 25 and 45° correspond to graphitic structure, indicating a low degree of graphitization, in accordance with the result of Raman spectrum of CMK-3 (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>B). After infiltrating red P into CMK-3, P@CMK-3 shows the XRD pattern of simple combination of CMK-3 and amorphous red P. In addition, both XRD peaks and Raman peaks (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>B) of red P in P@CMK-3 are weaker than that of pure red P, which could attributed to the confinement of nanosized red P in CMK-3. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The intensity ration of the D band to G band of P@CMK-3 is almost the same as that of CMK-3, and no more peaks are found for P@CMK-3 except these of CMK-3 and red P, indicating no interaction or bonding formed between red P and CMK-3. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a></p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/medium/nl-2015-03903d_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (A) XRD patterns and (B) Raman spectra of P@CMK-3, CMK-3, and commercial Red P. In Raman spectra, bands centered at ∼1348 and ∼1589 cm<sup>–1</sup> were presented as D-band and G-band, revealing defects and disorder portions and the graphitic layers, respectively. The <i>R</i><sub>I</sub> (<i>R</i><sub>I</sub> = <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) value corresponds the disorder degree of carbon (0.948 and 0.938 for CMK-3 and P@CMK-3, respectively).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The electrochemical performance of P@CMK-3 was tested for LIBs and NIBs, respectively. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>A,B displays cyclic voltammograms (CV) curves of P@CMK-3 composite in LIBs and NIBs, respectively. For LIBs, only one clear cathodic peak at ∼0.2 V appears in the first discharge process, ascribed to the activation process of red P during lithium insertion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref28" aria-label="reference 22 and 28">(22, 28)</a> Afterward, P@CMK-3 shows three cathodic peaks at ∼0.65, 0.33, and 0.01 V. The first two peaks are ascribed to the lithiation process to form Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>P (<i>x</i> = 1–3), the last peak at 0.01 V results from the lithium insertion into CMK-3. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref39" aria-label="reference 22 and 39">(22, 39)</a> For the anodic scan, three peaks at 1.1, 1.3, and 1.65 V could reveal the stepwise delithiation process from lithium insertion state to extraction state of P, combined with one weak peak at ∼0.2 V corresponding to the lithium extraction from CMK-3. The high reversibility of CV curves after the first cycle indicates good cycling stability of P@CMK-3 in LIBs. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>B shows the CV curves of P@CMK-3 for NIBs. For the anodic scan, a stepwise sodium extraction from Na<sub>3</sub>P also generates three peaks at 0.52, 0.7, and 1.42 V, the same as that in LIBs. In the first cathodic scan, only one broad and large peak between 0.45 and 0 V is observed, except an irreversible peak at ∼0.7 V in the first discharge process belonging to the decomposition of electrolyte to form solid electrolyte interface (SEI). Afterward, a cathodic peak at ∼0.35 V is developed at the third cycle with further sodiation of red P at ∼0.001 V, an indication of enhanced sodiation kinetics, in accordance with enhanced desodiation kinetics ascribed to the increased peak current in the subsequent anodic scans. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a></p><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/medium/nl-2015-03903d_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Cyclic voltammograms of P@CMK-3 at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs (A) and NIBs (B). Voltage profiles of P@CMK-3 electrode for (C) LIBs cycled between 0.001 and 2.5 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li at a cycling rate of 0.25 C and (D) NIBs cycled between 0.001 and 2.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at a cycling rate of 0.2 C. The capacity here was calculated based on the mass of red P.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>C represents the voltage profiles of P@CMK-3 for LIBs cycled at a current density of 0.25 C between 0.001 and 2.5 V vs (Li<sup>+</sup>/Li), corresponding to the typical characteristic of red P. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref23" aria-label="reference 16 and 23">(16, 23)</a> The P@CMK-3 delivers initial charge capacity of 1128.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of the composite, while the CMK-3 shows initial charge capacity of 499.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.25 C. So the specific capacity contribution of red P in P@CMK-3 is 2495 ((1128.6 – 499.2 × 0.6846)/0.3154) mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P, presenting an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of ∼68.2%. The irreversible capacity of the first cycle mainly results from the formation of SEI and irreversible lithium insertion into CMK-3 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4A</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> For the voltage profiles in NIBs (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>D), P@CMK-3 is tested at 0.2 C with a voltage window between 0.001 and 2.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Compare with lithium storage, it shows a lower voltage platform and capacity, which is attributed to the difference between the thermodynamics and kinetics of lithiation and sodiation process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref41" aria-label="reference 4 and 41">(4, 41)</a> The SEI formation and irreversible sodiation in CMK-3 also lead to a low ICE of 59.4% for NIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4B</a>). In the first cycle, the P@CMK-3 composite and CMK-3 can deliver initial charge capacity of 965.6 and 235.98 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 C, presenting a high specific capacity (2549 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P). After the initial capacity loss, the CE for both of P@CMK-3 for LIBs and NIBs increase to almost 100%, indicating excellent reversibility for lithium and sodium storage.</p><p><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>A,B investigates the cycle performance of the P@CMK-3 at current densities of 0.25C and 0.2 C for LIBs and NIBs, respectively. The P@CMK-3 shows excellent cycling stability in both LIBs and NIBs, where the capacity is based on the mass of red P. For LIBs, the P@CMK-3 delivers an initial reversible capacity of 2495 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, gradually increasing to 2574 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 4 cycles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>A), very close to the theoretical capacity of P (2595 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). After 85 cycles, the specific capacity of P@CMK-3 based on red P can remain ∼2240 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (the capacity based on the composite mass reaches 971 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 85 cycles) with a low capacity deterioration rate of less than 0.1% per cycle compared with the initial charge capacity. In case of Na storage, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>B, the P@CMK-3 displays good cycle stability from the second cycle onward, delivering discharge capacities based on red P of 2591, 2451, 2336, and 2188 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the second, 20th, 40th, and 60th cycle at 0.2 C, respectively. Base on the total mass of the composite, it can deliver 795 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 60 cycles. To further demonstrate the excellent electrochemical performance of P@CMK-3, the capacity contribution from CMK-3 is also measured under identical test conditions as shown in Figure S4C,D (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The CMK-3 delivers only reversible capacities of 380 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 150 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for Li<sup>+</sup> storage and Na<sup>+</sup> storage, respectively. Increasing mass loading of electrodes to 2.2 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, the P@CMK-3 composite still showed good cycle stability with a high capacity retention of 92.45% after 45 cycles at 0.25 C in LIBs and 83% after 40 cycles at 0.2 C in NIBs (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S5), an indication of promising application prospect. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 and 22">(20, 22)</a> In addition, the weight content of phosphorus in the P@CMK-3 composite was increased to ∼60 wt % to confirm the stability of the structure, which also displays improved lithium storage performance of 1440 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (based on the mass of the composite) at a current density of 200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S6). In addition, by comparing the current work with higher P content with previous works, P@CMK-3 still shows the better cycle performance (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Table S1).</p><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/medium/nl-2015-03903d_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical performance of P@CMK-3 composite for LIBs and NIBs cycled between 0.001 and 2.5 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li and 0.001 and 2.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. Capacity-cycle number curves of P@CMK-3 electrode at a cycling rate of 0.25 C for LIBs (A) and 0.2 C for NIBs (B); (C, D) Capacity of P@CMK-3 composite as a function of discharge rate (0.6–12 C for LIBs and 0.6–9.8 C for NIBs). The capacities here are calculated based on the mass of red P and the composite, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The rate capability of the anode material is an important performance when used for practical application. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>C,D shows the rate performance of P@CMK-3 for both LIBs and NIBs, respectively. In case of lithium storage, with the gradually increased current densities, P@CMK-3 displays capacities based on the mass of red P of 2320, 2185, 1985, 1930, 1783, 1598, 1200, and 934 at the current densities of 0.6, 1.2, 2.4, 3.6, 4.8, 6.1, 8.5, and 9.8C, respectively. Even at a high current rate of 12C, a stable capacity of 624 mA g h<sup>–1</sup> is still maintained, indicating the embedding the red P in CMK-3 is a promising strategy. As for NIBs, the specific capacity based on the mass of red P of P@CMK-3 is 2331, 2162, 1984, 1860, 1718, 1500, 1042, 808, and 650 mA g h<sup>–1</sup> when the current densities increase from 0.6, 1.2, 2.4, 3.6, 4.8, 6.1, 7.3, 8.5 to 9.8 C. The capacities referring to the P@CMK-3 composite, CMK-3, and red P are presented in the Tables S2 and S3 (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>C,D, when calculating the capacities based on the composite for both LIBs and NIBs, it also delivers excellent rate performance with high capacity retention at higher current densities. Furthermore, the long cycling behavior of P@CMK-3 composite at high current density was examined. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>A shows the lithium storage performance of P@CMK-3 at 1.2 C and 5 C. It displays a reversible charge capacity of 1798 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, with the capacity retention of ∼80% after 1000 cycles at 1.2 C. Even cycled at 5 C, a reversible capacity of 1150 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained after 1000 cycles. When used as anode for NIBs (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>B), it can also deliver a high specific capacity of 1600 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 140 cycles at 1 C and 1020 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 210 cycles at 5 C, respectively. All of the capacities here are based on the mass of red P. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7A,B</a> shows the capacity retention calculated based on the total mass of the composite and excellent electrochemical performance at 1.2 and 5 C for LIBs (583 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 800 cycles at 1.2 C and 500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 5 C), and 1 and 5 C for NIBs (580 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 140 cycles at 1 C and 370 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 210 cycles at 5 C). To the best of our knowledge, excellent lithium storage and sodium storage performance of the P@CMK-3 is unprecedented. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> The electrochemical activity of phosphorus (related to the utilization of phosphorus) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> in P@CMK-3 for both LIBs and NIBs at higher current densities is superior to several phosphorus-based composite reported recently (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>C,D), including red phosphorus/carbon nanofibers composite, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> phosphorus/graphene composites, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref22 ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref22 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 17, 22, 24 and 25">(17, 22, 24, 25)</a> amorphous phosphorus/carbon composites, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref38" aria-label="reference 29 and 38">(29, 38)</a> nanostructured phosphorus/porous carbon composites, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> and red phosphorus–carbon nanotube composites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> The exceptional electrochemical performance of the composite is mainly attributed to the novel structure, nanostructured phosphorus confined in highly ordered mesoporous carbon. In accordance with the schematic illustration in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>, the highly ordered mesopores in CMK-3 remain stable after lithiation and sodiation and successfully protect red P from fracture and separation from conductive substrates (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figures S8 and S9), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43, 44)</a> and limit the size of red P to ∼4 nm, leading to short transport length of ions/electrons along the radial direction of the channels of CMK-3 and the nanosized red P (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S10). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45, 46)</a> In addition, conductive mesoporous CMK-3 matrix enhances ionic and electronic conductivity, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> which can be proved by the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S11A,B). The high frequency semicircles of the composite in LIBs and NIBs after several cycles remain low value, indicating fast Li/Na ions reaction between nanostructured red P and high electron transport rate during the cycle life. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref21" aria-label="reference 9 and 21">(9, 21)</a> By fitting the impedance spectra to an equivalent electrical circuit, including ohmic resistance of batteries (<i>R</i><sub>i</sub>), surface film resistance and charge transfer at the interface between the electrode and electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>sf+ct</sub>) in parallel with a constant phase element (CPE<sub>sf+ct</sub>), and Warburg impedance (<i>W</i><sub>o</sub>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> For LIBs, the combined resistance <i>R</i><sub>sf+ct</sub> is almost the same (57 Ω) after 10 and 20 cycles, only increasing to 68 Ω after 30 cycles; the <i>R</i><sub>sf+ct</sub> for NIBs after 10, 20, and 30 cycles is 112, 117, and 126 Ω, respectively. The low resistance increase and the almost overlapped Nyquist plots indicate that the formed solid electrolyte interface (SEI) during the initial cycles is stable after subsequent cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref24" aria-label="reference 20 and 24">(20, 24)</a> The structure stability of the composite is beneficial to the formation of stable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer during cycling (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/suppl_file/nl5b03903_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S11C,D), maintaining nearly 100% Coulombic efficiency and excellent cyclability.</p><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/medium/nl-2015-03903d_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Excellent cycle performance of P@CMK-3 electrodes for LIBs at 1.2 and 5 C (A) and NIBs at 1 and 5C (B) with activation first at low current density. The capacities here are calculated based on the mass of red P; comparison of utilization of phosphorus in several phosphorus-based composites at higher current densities reported recently for LIBs (C) and NIBs (D). (The comparison of utilization of phosphorus in composites is based on the specific capacity contribution of phosphorus in composites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> For some works that did not provide the specific capacity contribution of phosphorus, we assume that the capacity contribution of carbon matrix in the composites at higher current densities is negligible and present as high specific capacity of phosphorus as possible. The utilization of phosphorus is calculated by the following equation as: Utilization of phosphorus = [specific capacity contribution of phosphorus/theoretical capacity of phosphorus (2595 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>)] × 100%.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903/asset/images/large/nl-2015-03903d_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03903&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="last">In summary, we have developed a facile strategy to prepare nanosized amorphous red phosphorus confined in CMK-3 (P@CMK-3) through a vaporization–condensation–conversion process. The porous carbon matrix (CMK-3) offers intrinsic open channel structure and ensures high ionic/electronic conductivity. The intimate contact between the CMK-3 and nanosized red P suppresses the growth and agglomeration of red P, leading to reduced diffusion length and fast lithium/sodium storage. Owing to this unique structure, the P@CMK-3 demonstrated a high reversible specific capacity of ∼2250 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P at 0.25 C (∼971 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the composite), excellent rate performance of 1598 and 624 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P at 6.1 and 12 C, respectively (562 and 228 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of the composite at 6.1 and 12 C, respectively), and significantly enhanced cycle life of 1150 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P at 5 C (500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of the composite) after 1000 cycles for LIBs. As anode for NIBs, a specific capacity of 1020 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of red P (370 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of the composite) is obtained after 210 cycles at 5 C. The present study clearly demonstrates the feasibility to use red P as a high-performance anode for high-power LIBs and NIBs.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info"                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Designing the next generation high capacity battery electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, H.-C.; Ling, C.; Bhattacharya, J.; Thomas, J. C.; Thornton, K.; Van der Ven, A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1760-1768</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Much of current research in electrochem. energy storage is devoted to new electrode chemistries and reaction mechanisms that promise substantial increases in energy d.  Unfortunately, most high capacity electrodes exhibit an unacceptably large hysteresis in their voltage profile.  Using a first-principles multi-scale approach to examine particle level dynamics, we identify intrinsic thermodn. and kinetic properties that are responsible for the large hysteresis exhibited by many high capacity electrodes.  Our anal. shows that the hysteresis in the voltage profile of high capacity electrodes that rely on displacement reactions arises from a difference in reaction paths between charge and discharge.  We demonstrate that different reaction paths are followed (i) when there is a large mismatch in ionic mobilities between the electrochem. active species (e.g. Li) and displaced ionic species and (ii) when there is a lack of a thermodn. driving force to redistribute displaced ions upon charging of the electrode.  These insights motivate the formulation of design metrics for displacement reactions in terms of fundamental properties detd. by the chem. and crystallog. of the electrode material, properties that are now readily accessible with first-principles computation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/c1ee01598b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Etacheri, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Marom, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Elazari, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Salitra, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Aurbach, D.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.
